<p>The <b>Christadelphians</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>) are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millenarian" title="Millenarian">millenarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> group who hold a view of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Unitarianism" title="Biblical Unitarianism">Biblical Unitarianism</a>. There are approximately 50,000 Christadelphians in around 120 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The movement developed in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> in the 19th century around the teachings of <a href="/wiki/John_Thomas_(Christadelphian)" title="John Thomas (Christadelphian)">John Thomas</a>, who coined the name <i>Christadelphian</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRWname_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-BRWname-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StrongName_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-StrongName-3">[3]</a></sup> from the Greek for "Brethren in Christ".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ourname_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ourname-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LippyName_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-LippyName-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Basing their beliefs solely on the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, Christadelphians differ from mainstream <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in a number of doctrinal areas. For example, they reject the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortality_of_the_soul" title="Immortality of the soul">immortality of the soul</a>, believing these to be corruptions of original Christian teaching. They were initially found predominantly in the developed English-speaking world, but expanded in developing countries after the Second World War. Congregations are traditionally referred to as 'ecclesias' and would not use the word 'church' due to its association with mainstream Christianity, although today it is more acceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Christadelphian religious group traces its origins to <a href="/wiki/John_Thomas_(Christadelphian)" title="John Thomas (Christadelphian)">John Thomas</a> (1805–1871), who emigrated to North America from England in 1832.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch3_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch3-8">[8]</a></sup> Following a near shipwreck he vowed to find out the truth about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_existence" title="Human existence">life</a> and God through personal <a href="/wiki/Bible_study_(Christian)" title="Bible study (Christian)">Biblical study</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch3_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch3-8">[8]</a></sup> Initially he sought to avoid the kind of <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">sectarianism</a> he had seen in England. In this he found sympathy with the rapidly emerging <a href="/wiki/Restoration_Movement" title="Restoration Movement">Restoration Movement</a> in the US at the time. This movement sought a reform based upon the <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">Bible alone</a> as a sufficient guide and rejected all <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>. However, this liberality eventually led to dissent as John Thomas developed his personal beliefs and began to question mainstream orthodox Christian beliefs. Whilst the Restoration Movement accepted Thomas's right to have his own beliefs, when he started preaching that they were essential to <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a>, it led to a fierce series of debates with a notable <a href="/wiki/Leadership" title="Leadership">leader</a> of the movement, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Campbell_(clergyman)" title="Alexander Campbell (clergyman)">Alexander Campbell</a>. John Thomas believed that scripture, as God's word, did not support a multiplicity of differing beliefs, and challenged the leaders to continue with the process of restoring 1st-century Christian beliefs and correct interpretation through a process of debate. The history of this process appears in the book <i>Dr. Thomas, His Life and Work</i> (1873) by a Christadelphian, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Roberts_(Christadelphian)" title="Robert Roberts (Christadelphian)">Robert Roberts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this period of formulating his ideas John Thomas was <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a> twice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> the second time after renouncing the beliefs he previously held. He based his new position on a new appreciation for the reign of Christ on David's throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The abjuration of his former beliefs eventually led to the Restoration Movement disfellowshipping him when he toured England and they became aware of his abjuration in the United States of America.</p>
<p>The Christadelphian community in Britain effectively dates from Thomas's first lecturing tour (May 1848 – October 1850). His message was particularly welcomed in Scotland, and <a href="/wiki/Campbellite" title="Campbellite">Campbellite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventist</a> friends separated to form groups of "Baptised Believers". Two thirds of ecclesias, and members, in Britain before 1864 were in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1849, during his tour of Britain, he completed (a decade and a half before the name <i>Christadelphian</i> was conceived) <i><a href="/wiki/Elpis_Israel" title="Elpis Israel">Elpis Israel</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> in which he laid out his understanding of the main doctrines of the Bible.</p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Elpis_Israel" title="wikisource:Elpis Israel">Elpis Israel</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Since his medium for bringing change was print and debate, it was natural for the origins of the Christadelphian body to be associated with books and journals, such as Thomas's <i>Herald of the Kingdom</i>.</p>
<p>In his desire to seek to establish Biblical truth and test orthodox Christian beliefs through independent scriptural study he was not alone. Among other churches, he had links with <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventist</a> movement and with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benjamin_Wilson_(Biblical_scholar)" title="Benjamin Wilson (Biblical scholar)">Benjamin Wilson</a> (who later set up the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Blessed_Hope" title="Church of the Blessed Hope">Church of God of the Abrahamic Faith</a> in the 1860s). In terms of his rejection of the trinity, Thomas' views had certain similarities with Unitarianism which had developed in a formal way in Europe in the 16th century (although he formally described both Unitarianism and Socinianism as "works of the devil" for their failure to develop his doctrine of God-manifestation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/History_of_Unitarianism" title="History of Unitarianism">History of Unitarianism</a></p>
<p>Although the Christadelphian movement originated through the activities of John Thomas, he never saw himself as making his own disciples. He believed rather that he had rediscovered 1st Century beliefs from the Bible alone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and sought to prove that through a process of challenge and debate and writing journals. Through that process a number of people became convinced and set up various fellowships that had sympathy with that position. Groups associated with John Thomas met under various names, including Believers, Baptised Believers, the Royal Association of Believers, Baptised Believers in the Kingdom of God, Nazarines (or Nazarenes) and The Antipas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anrvrv_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-anrvrv-18">[18]</a></sup> until the time of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–1865). At that time, church affiliation was required in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and in the Confederacy in order to register for <a href="/wiki/Conscientious_objector" title="Conscientious objector">conscientious objector</a> status, and in 1864 Thomas chose for registration purposes the name <i>Christadelphian</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRWname_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-BRWname-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StrongName_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-StrongName-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ourname_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ourname-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LippyName_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-LippyName-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through the teaching of John Thomas and the need in the American Civil War for a name, the Christadelphians emerged as a denomination, but they were formed into a lasting structure through a passionate follower of Thomas's interpretation of the Bible, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Roberts_(Christadelphian)" title="Robert Roberts (Christadelphian)">Robert Roberts</a>. In 1864 he began to publish <i>The Ambassador of the Coming Age</i> magazine. This was renamed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christadelphian" title="The Christadelphian">The Christadelphian</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> in 1869 and continues to be published under that name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Roberts was prominent in the period following the death of John Thomas in 1871, and helped craft the structures of the Christadelphian body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Robert Roberts was certain that John Thomas had rediscovered the truth. Robert Robert's life was characterised by debates over issues that arose within the fledgling organisation; some of these debates can be found in the book <i>Robert Roberts—A study of his life and character</i> by Islip Collyer.</p>
<p>Initially the denomination grew in the English-speaking world, particularly in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Midlands" title="English Midlands">English Midlands</a> and in parts<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2013)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> of North America. In the early days after the death of John Thomas the group could have moved in a number of directions. Doctrinal issues arose, debates took place and statements of faith were created and amended as other issues arose. These attempts were felt necessary by many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to both settle and define a doctrinal stance for the newly emerging denomination and to keep out error. As a result of these debates, several groups separated from the main body of Christadelphians, most notably the Suffolk Street fellowship and the Unamended fellowship.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Christadelphian position on conscientious objection came to the fore with the introduction of conscription during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>. Varying degrees of exemption from military service were granted to Christadelphians in the United Kingdom, Canada, Australia, New Zealand and the United States. In the Second World War, this frequently required the person seeking exemption to undertake civilian work under the direction of the authorities.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> the Christadelphians in Britain assisted in the <a href="/wiki/Kindertransport" title="Kindertransport">Kindertransport</a>, helping to relocate several hundred Jewish children away from Nazi persecution and founding a hostel <a href="/wiki/Elpis_Lodge" title="Elpis Lodge">Elpis Lodge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In Germany the small Christadelphian community founded by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Maier" title="Albert Maier">Albert Maier</a> went underground from 1940–1945, and a leading brother, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Merz" title="Albert Merz">Albert Merz</a>, was imprisoned as a conscientious objector and later executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Second World War, moves were taken to try to reunite various of the earlier divisions. By the end of the 1950s, most Christadelphians had united into one community, but there are still a number of small groups who remain separate.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Today">Today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The post-war, and post-reunions, period saw an increase in co-operation and interaction between ecclesias, resulting in the establishment of a number of week-long Bible schools and the formation of national and <a href="/wiki/International" title="International">international</a> organisations such as the Christadelphian Bible Mission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> (for preaching and pastoral support overseas), the Christadelphian Support Network<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> (for counselling), and the <a href="/wiki/Meal-a-Day_Fund" title="Meal-a-Day Fund">Christadelphian Meal-A-Day Fund</a> (for charity and humanitarian work).</p>
<p>The period following the reunions was accompanied by expansion in the developing world, which now accounts for around 40% of Christadelphians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_organization">General organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: General organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the absence of centralised organization, some differences exist amongst Christadelphians on matters of belief and practice. This is because each congregation (commonly styled 'ecclesias') is organized <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/autonomous" title="wiktionary:autonomous">autonomously</a>, typically following common practices which have altered little since the 19th century. Most ecclesias have a constitution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> which includes a 'Statement of Faith', a list of 'Doctrines to be Rejected' and a formalized list of 'The Commandments of Christ'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> With no central authority, individual congregations are responsible for maintaining orthodoxy in belief and practice, and the statement of faith is seen by many as useful to this end. The statement of faith acts as the official standard of most ecclesias to determine fellowship within and between ecclesias, and as the basis for co-operation between ecclesias. Congregational discipline and conflict resolution are applied using various forms of consultation, mediation, and discussion, with disfellowship (similar to <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a>) being the final response to those with unorthodox practices or beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The relative uniformity of organization and practice is undoubtedly due to the influence of a booklet, written early in Christadelphian history by Robert Roberts, called <i>A Guide to the Formation and Conduct of Christadelphian Ecclesias</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-online-31">[31]</a></sup> It recommends a basically democratic arrangement by which congregational members elect 'brothers' to arranging and serving duties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and includes guidelines for the organization of committees, as well as conflict resolution between congregational members and between congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Christadelphians do not have paid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_religion" title="Minister of religion">ministers</a>. Male members are assessed by the congregation for their eligibility to teach and perform other duties, which are usually assigned on a rotation basis, as opposed to having a permanently appointed preacher. Congregational governance typically follows a <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> model, with an elected arranging committee for each individual ecclesia. This unpaid committee is responsible for the day-to-day running of the ecclesia and is answerable to the rest of the ecclesia's members.</p>
<p>Inter-ecclesial organizations co-ordinate the running of, among other things, Christadelphian schools<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and elderly care homes, the <a href="/wiki/Christadelphian_Isolation_League" title="Christadelphian Isolation League">Christadelphian Isolation League</a> (which cares for those prevented by distance or infirmity from attending an ecclesia regularly) and the publication of <a href="/wiki/Category:Christadelphian_magazines" title="Category:Christadelphian magazines">Christadelphian magazines</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherents">Adherents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Adherents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>No official membership figures are published, but the <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Encyclopedia" title="Columbia Encyclopedia">Columbia Encyclopedia</a></i> gives an estimated figure of 50,000 Christadelphians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exrydu_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-exrydu-35">[35]</a></sup> They are spread across approximately 120 countries;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xbcrcq_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-xbcrcq-36">[36]</a></sup> there are established churches (often referred to as <i>ecclesias</i>) in many of those countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> along with isolated members. Estimates for the main centers of Christadelphian population are as follows: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (18,000),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (10,653),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (7,500),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> (7,000),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (6,500),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (3,375),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> (1,785),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (1,750),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> (1,700),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> (1,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (900).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-45">[45]</a></sup> Combining the estimates from the Christadelphian Bible Mission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-45">[45]</a></sup> with the figures above, the numbers for each continent are as follows: Africa (21,400), Americas (10,500), Asia (4,150), Australasia (12,600), Europe (18,950). This puts the total figure at around 67,000.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fellowships">Fellowships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fellowships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Christadelphian body consists of a number of <i>fellowships</i> – groups of ecclesias which associate with one another, often to the exclusion of ecclesias outside their group. They are to some degree localised. The Unamended Fellowship, for example, exists only in North America. Christadelphian fellowships have often been named after ecclesias or magazines who took a lead in developing a particular stance.</p>
<p>The majority of Christadelphians (around 60,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>) belong to <i>Central fellowship</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birmingham_1933.2C_p._376_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birmingham_1933.2C_p._376-49">[49]</a></sup> named after the Birmingham Central ecclesia. This was formed in 1957–1958 as a result of a reunion between the Temperance Hall and Suffolk Street fellowships in the UK. The <i>Suffolk Street fellowship</i> had formed in 1885 over the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">inspiration</a> of the Bible. Robert Ashcroft, a leading member, wrote an article which challenged Christadelphian belief in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/plenary" title="wiktionary:plenary">plenary</a> inspiration and which, although he himself left, led to a division in the main body. One group formed a new ecclesia which later met in Suffolk Street, Birmingham. Other ecclesias throughout the world which supported them became known as the "Suffolk Street fellowship" to distinguish them from the group they had separated from, which became known as the "Temperance Hall fellowship". The main magazine of this group from 1884–1957 was <i>The Fraternal Visitor</i>, whose editors included J.J. Bishop and J.J. Hadley (d. 1912), then <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Turner_(metallurgist)" title="Thomas Turner (metallurgist)">Thomas Turner</a>, and finally Cyril Cooper (till reunion in 1957). Reunion in the United Kingdom (Temperance Hall-Suffolk Street) was closely followed by reunion in Australia in 1958 between the Shield fellowship (aligned with the Suffolk Street fellowship) and the Central fellowship on the basis of an understanding of the atonement expressed in a document called the Cooper-Carter Addendum (to the BASF). The Central fellowship in North America is often referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Amended_Christadelphians" title="Amended Christadelphians">Amended fellowship</a></i>.</p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Unamended_Christadelphians" title="Unamended Christadelphians">Unamended fellowship</a></i>, consisting of around 1,850 members, is found in East Coast and Midwest USA and Ontario, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This group separated in 1898 as a result of differing views on who would be raised to judgment at the return of Christ. The majority of Christadelphians believe that the judgment will include anyone who had sufficient knowledge of the gospel message, and is not limited to baptized believers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The majority in Britain, Australia and North America amended their statement of faith accordingly. Those who opposed the amendment became known as the "Unamended fellowship" and allowed the teaching that God either could not or would not raise those who had no covenant relationship with him. Opinions vary as to what the established position was on this subject prior to the controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Prominent in the formation of the Unamended fellowship was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Williams_(Christadelphian)" title="Thomas Williams (Christadelphian)">Thomas Williams</a>, editor of the Christadelphian Advocate magazine. The majority of the Unamended Fellowship outside North America joined the Suffolk Street fellowship before its eventual incorporation into Central fellowship. There is also some co-operation between the Central (Amended) and Unamended Fellowships in North America – most recently in the Great Lakes region, where numerous Amended and Unamended ecclesias have opened fellowship to one another despite the failure of wider attempts at re-union under the North American Statement of Understanding (NASU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Berean_Christadelphians" title="Berean Christadelphians">Berean Fellowship</a></i> was formed in 1923 as a result of varying views on military service in Britain, and on the atonement in North America. The majority of the North American Bereans re-joined the main body of Christadelphians in 1952. A number continue as a separate community, numbering around 200 in Texas, 100 in Kenya and 30 in Wales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Most of the divisions still in existence within the Christadelphian community today stem from further divisions of the Berean fellowship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1942 the Berean fellowship divided over marriage and divorce with the stricter party forming the <i>Dawn fellowship</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Following union with the Lightstand fellowship in Australia in November 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> there are now 800 members in the UK, Australia, Canada, India, Jamaica, Poland, the Philippines and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Old Paths fellowship</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> was formed in the 1957 by those in the Temperance Hall fellowship who held that the reasons for separation from the Suffolk Street fellowship remained and opposed the re-union. There are around 250 members in the UK, and 150 in Australasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other Christadelphian Fellowships,with lesser numbers of members,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> include the <i>Watchman fellowship</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> the <i>Companion fellowship</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and the <i>Pioneer Fellowship</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Blessed_Hope" title="Church of the Blessed Hope">Church of God of the Abrahamic Faith (CGAF)</a> has common origins with Christadelphians and shares Christadelphian beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Numbering around 400 (primarily Ohio and Florida, USA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> they are welcomed into fellowship by some Christadelphians and are currently involved in unity talks.</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bryan_R._Wilson" title="Bryan R. Wilson">Bryan Wilson</a>, functionally the definition of a "fellowship" within Christadelphian history has been mutual or unilateral exclusion of groupings of ecclesias from the breaking of bread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This functional definition still holds true in North America, where the Unamended fellowship and the Church of God of the Abrahamic Faith are not received by most North American <a href="/wiki/Amended_Christadelphians" title="Amended Christadelphians">Amended</a> ecclesias. But outside North America this functional definition no longer holds. Many articles and books on the doctrine and practice of fellowship now reject the notion itself of separate "fellowships" among those who recognise the same baptism, viewing such separations as schismatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Many ecclesias in the Central fellowship would not refuse a baptised Christadelphian from a minority fellowship from breaking bread; the exclusion is more usually the other way.</p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Birmingham_Amended_Statement_of_Faith" title="wikisource:Birmingham Amended Statement of Faith">Birmingham Amended Statement of Faith</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>They tend to operate organisationally fairly similarly, although there are different emphases. Despite their differences, the Central, Old Paths, Dawn<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and Berean<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> fellowships generally subscribe to the <i>Birmingham Amended Statement of Faith</i> (BASF), though the latter two have additional clauses or supporting documents to explain their position. Most Unamended ecclesias use the <i>Birmingham Unamended Statement of Faith</i> (BUSF)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> with one clause being different. Within the Central fellowship individual ecclesias also may have their own statement of faith, whilst still accepting the statement of faith of the larger community. Some ecclesias have statements around their positions, especially on divorce and re-marriage, making clear that offence would be caused by anyone in that position seeking to join them at the 'Breaking of Bread' service. Others tolerate a degree of divergence from commonly held Christadelphian views.</p>
<p>For each fellowship, anyone who publicly assents to the doctrines described in the statement and is in good standing in their "home ecclesia" is generally welcome to participate in the activities of any other ecclesia.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Due to the way the Christadelphian body is organised there is no central authority to establish and maintain a standardised set of beliefs and it depends what statement of faith is adhered to and how liberal the ecclesia is, but there are core doctrines most Christadelphians would accept. In the formal statements of faith a more complete list is found. For instance in the Central fellowship, the BASF, the standard statement of faith, has 30 doctrines to be accepted and 35 to be rejected.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bible">The Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christadelphians state that their beliefs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> are based wholly on the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hyndman_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-hyndman-73">[73]</a></sup> and they do not see other works as inspired by <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> They regard the Bible as inspired by God and, therefore, believe that, in its original form, it is error free and errors in later copies are due to errors of transcription or translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Based on this, Christadelphians teach what they believe as true Bible teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="God">God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christadelphians believe that God is the creator of all things and the father of true believers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> that he is a separate being from his son, <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> Christ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-trinity1-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup> and that the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit#Christadelphians" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> is the power of God used in creation and for <a href="/wiki/Salvation#Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spirit_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-81">[81]</a></sup> They also believe that the phrase <i>Holy Spirit</i> sometimes refers to God's character/mind, depending on the context in which the phrase appears,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup> but reject the view that we need strength, guidance and power from the Holy Spirit to live the Christian life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> believing instead that the spirit a believer needs within themselves is the mind/character of God, which is developed in a believer by their reading of the Bible (which, they believe, contains words God gave by his Spirit) and trying to live by what it says during the events of their lives which God uses to help shape their character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christadelphian_Hall_(Bath).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG/220px-Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG/330px-Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG/440px-Christadelphian_Hall_%28Bath%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christadelphian_Hall_(Bath).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Christadelphian Hall in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>, United Kingdom</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jesus">Jesus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Jesus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christadelphians believe that Jesus is the promised Jewish <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>, in whom the prophecies and promises of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> find their fulfilment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zilmer_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-zilmer-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> They believe he is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Son_of_Man" title="Son of Man">Son of Man</a>, in that he inherited human nature (with its inclination to sin) from his mother, and the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a> by virtue of his miraculous conception by the power of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zilmer_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-zilmer-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devil_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-devil-88">[88]</a></sup> Although he was tempted, Jesus committed no sin, and was therefore a perfect representative sacrifice to bring salvation to sinful humankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zilmer_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-zilmer-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devil_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-devil-88">[88]</a></sup> They believe that God raised Jesus from death and gave him <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a>, and he ascended to <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">Heaven</a>, God's dwelling place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zilmer_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-zilmer-86">[86]</a></sup> Christadelphians believe that he will return to the earth in person to set up the Kingdom of God in fulfilment of the promises made to <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_(biblical_king)" title="David (biblical king)">David</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This includes the belief that the coming Kingdom will be the restoration of God's first Kingdom of Israel, which was under David and Solomon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-93">[93]</a></sup> For Christadelphians, this is the focal point of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a> taught by Jesus and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">apostles</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvation">Salvation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Salvation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christadelphians believe that people are separated from God because of their sins but that mankind can be reconciled to him by becoming disciples of Jesus Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salvation_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-salvation-95">[95]</a></sup> This is by belief in the gospel, through <a href="/wiki/Repentance" title="Repentance">repentance</a>, and through <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> by total immersion in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salvation_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-salvation-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baptism_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-baptism-96">[96]</a></sup> They do not believe we can be sure of being saved, believing instead that salvation comes as a result of a life of obedience to the commands of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> After death, believers are in a state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychopannychism" title="Psychopannychism">non-existence</a>, knowing nothing until the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">Resurrection</a> at the return of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Following the judgement at that time, the accepted receive the gift of <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a>, and live with Christ on a restored Earth, assisting him to establish the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> and to rule over the mortal population for a thousand years (the <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">Millennium</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Christadelphians believe that the Kingdom will be centred upon Israel, but Jesus Christ will also reign over all the other nations on the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Some believe that the Kingdom itself is not worldwide but limited to the land of Israel promised to Abraham and ruled over in the past by David, with a worldwide empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_in_Christ">Life in Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Life in Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Commandments_of_Christ" title="wikisource:The Commandments of Christ">The Commandments of Christ</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The historic <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/the_Commandments_of_Christ" title="wikisource:the Commandments of Christ">Commandments of Christ</a></i> demonstrates the community's recognition of the importance of Biblical teaching on morality. Marriage and family life are important. Christadelphians believe that sexual relationships should be limited to heterosexual marriage, ideally between baptised believers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Similarities_and_differences_with_other_Christians">Similarities and differences with other Christians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Similarities and differences with other Christians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disagreement_with_some_mainstream_doctrines">Disagreement with some mainstream doctrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Disagreement with some mainstream doctrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christadelphians reject a number of doctrines held by many other Christians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> notably the <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Christian_mortalism" title="Christian mortalism">mortalism</a>; <a href="/wiki/Christian_conditionalism" title="Christian conditionalism">conditionalism</a>), <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">trinitarianism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity1_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-trinity1-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup> the personal <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence_of_Christ" title="Pre-existence of Christ">pre-existence of Christ</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">baptism of infants</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baptism_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-baptism-96">[96]</a></sup> the personhood of the Holy Spirit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity1_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-trinity1-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spirit_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup> the divinity of Jesus and the present-day possession of the gifts of the Holy Spirit (see <a href="/wiki/Cessationism" title="Cessationism">cessationism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spirit_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zilmer_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-zilmer-86">[86]</a></sup> They believe that the word <i><a href="/wiki/Devil" title="Devil">devil</a></i> is a reference in the scriptures to sin and human nature in opposition to God, while the word <i><a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">satan</a></i> is merely a reference to an adversary (be it good or bad). According to Christadelphians, these terms are used in reference to specific political systems or individuals in opposition or conflict. <i><a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a></i> (Hebrew: Sheol; Greek: Hades, Gehenna) is understood to refer exclusively to death and the grave, rather than being a place of everlasting torment (see also <a href="/wiki/Annihilationism" title="Annihilationism">annihilationism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devil_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-devil-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Christadelphians do not believe that anyone will "go to Heaven" upon death. Instead, they believe that only Christ Jesus went to Heaven, and when he comes back to the earth there will be a <a href="/wiki/Resurrection#Christianity" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> and God's kingdom will be established on earth, starting in the land of Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-93">[93]</a></sup> Christadelphians believe the doctrines they reject were introduced into <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> after the 1st century in large part through exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">pagan Greek philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and cannot be substantiated from the Biblical texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity1_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-trinity1-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesus_80-6"><a href="#cite_note-jesus-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trinity2_82-5"><a href="#cite_note-trinity2-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_historical_groups_and_individuals_with_some_shared_doctrines">Other historical groups and individuals with some shared doctrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other historical groups and individuals with some shared doctrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One criticism of the Christadelphian movement has been over the claim of John Thomas and Robert Roberts to have "rediscovered" scriptural truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However one might argue that <i>all</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> groups make the same claims to some extent. Although both men believed that they had "recovered" the true doctrines for themselves and contemporaries, they also believed there had always existed a group of true believers throughout the ages, albeit marred by the apostasy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most notable Christadelphian attempts to find a continuity of those with doctrinal similarities since that point have been geographer <a href="/wiki/Alan_Eyre" title="Alan Eyre">Alan Eyre</a>'s two books <i>The Protesters</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> (1975) and <i>Brethren in Christ</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> (1982) in which he shows that many individual Christadelphian doctrines had been previously believed. Eyre focused in particular on the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a>, and also among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socinians" title="Socinians">Socinians</a> and other early <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Dissenters</a>. In this way, Eyre was able to demonstrate substantial historical precedents for individual Christadelphian teachings and practices, and believed that the Christadelphian community was the 'inheritor of a noble tradition, by which elements of the Truth were from century to century hammered out on the anvil of controversy, affliction and even anguish'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Eyre_page_8_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Eyre_page_8-114">[114]</a></sup> Although noting in the introduction to 'The Protestors' that 'Some recorded herein perhaps did not have "all the truth" — so the writer has been reminded',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Eyre_page_8_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Eyre_page_8-114">[114]</a></sup> Eyre nevertheless claimed that the purpose of the work was to 'tell how a number of little-known individuals, groups and religious communities strove to preserve or revive the original Christianity of apostolic times',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and that 'In faith and outlook they were far closer to the early springing shoots of 1st-century Christianity and the penetrating spiritual challenge of Jesus himself than much that has passed for the religion of the Nazarene in the last nineteen centuries'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eyre's research has been criticized by some of his Christadelphian peers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and as a result Christadelphian commentary on the subject has subsequently been more cautious and circumspect, with caveats being issued concerning Eyre's claims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and the two books less used and publicized than in previous years.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, even with most source writings of those later considered "heretics" destroyed, evidence can be provided that since the 1st century CE there have been various groups and individuals who have held certain individual Christadelphian beliefs or similar ones. For example, all the distinctive Christadelphian doctrines (with the exception of the non-literal devil),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-socinian_devil_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-socinian_devil-120">[120]</a></sup> down to interpretations of specific verses, can be found particularly among 16th century <a href="/wiki/Socinianism" title="Socinianism">Socinian</a> writers (e.g. the rejection of the doctrines of the trinity, <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence_of_Christ" title="Pre-existence of Christ">pre-existence of Christ</a>, immortal souls, a literal hell of fire, original sin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Early English Unitarian writings also correspond closely to those of Christadelphians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Also, recent discoveries and research have shown a large similarity between Christadelphian beliefs and those held by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton#Religious_views" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> who, among other things, rejected the doctrines of the trinity, immortal souls, a personal devil and literal demons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Further examples are as follows:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2015)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The typical Old Testament belief in unconsciousness till resurrection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> instead of the immortality of the soul, has been held marginally throughout the history of both Judaism and Christianity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> such sources include certain Jewish pseudepigraphal works,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> rabbinical works,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clement_of_Rome" title="Clement of Rome">Clement of Rome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Christadelphians#Dubious" title="Talk:Christadelphians">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Arnobius" title="Arnobius">Arnobius</a> in the 3rd to 4th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> a succession of Arabic and Syrian Christians from the 3rd to the 8th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ephrem_p._279_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ephrem_p._279-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ephrem_p._279_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ephrem_p._279-132">[132]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Aphrahat" title="Aphrahat">Aphrahat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ephrem_the_Syrian" title="Ephrem the Syrian">Ephrem</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Narsai" title="Narsai">Narsai</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_of_Nineveh" title="Isaac of Nineveh">Isaac of Nineveh</a> (d.700),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_of_Sarug" title="Jacob of Sarug">Jacob of Sarug</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Jewish commentators such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraham_Ibn_Ezra" title="Abraham Ibn Ezra">Abraham Ibn Ezra</a> (1092–1167),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a> (1135–1204),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Albo" title="Joseph Albo">Joseph Albo</a> (1380–1444),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and later Christians such as <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sattler" title="Michael Sattler">Michael Sattler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptists</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> long before <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> challenged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> views on heaven and hell with his teaching of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_sleep" title="Soul sleep">soul sleep</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Christadelphian denial of the <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence_of_Christ" title="Pre-existence of Christ">pre-existence of Christ</a>, and interpretation of verses such as "I came down from heaven" (John 6:38) as relating to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth</a> and Christ's mission only, are found in the teachings of: the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Christians" title="Jewish Christians">Jewish Christians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagner_2000_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagner_2000-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Christadelphians#Dubious" title="Talk:Christadelphians">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ebionites" title="Ebionites">Ebionites</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazoreans" title="Nazoreans">Nazoreans</a> (or Nazarenes),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (June 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Christadelphians#This_is_very_debatable.2C_since_Pritz.27s_monograph_on_the_Nazarenes_regards_their_Christology_as_orthodox._.28Pritz.2C_R._.281988.29._Nazarene_Jewish_Christianity._Leiden:_Brill.2C_p._35_n._8.29._An_article_on_Christadelphians_is_not_the_place_to_assume_a_disputed_reconstruction_of_early_Jewish_Christianity." title="Talk:Christadelphians">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> the Theodotians of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodotus_the_Cobbler" title="Theodotus the Cobbler">Theodotus the Cobbler</a> (who believed Jesus was supernaturally begotten but a man nonetheless),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artemon" title="Artemon">Artemon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Samosata" title="Paul of Samosata">Paul of Samosata</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudo-Clementines" title="Pseudo-Clementines">Pseudo-Clementines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagner_2000_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hagner_2000-145">[145]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Photinus" title="Photinus">Photinus</a> (d.376);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> naturally however, given that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Trinitarian" title="Non-Trinitarian">non-Trinitarian</a> beliefs were punishable with death from the 4th century to the 17th, it would be foolish to expect to discover any consistent line of people or groups holding such beliefs. Such attempts become possible only after the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>. Christadelphian Christology is found from the publication of <a href="/wiki/Lelio_Sozzini" title="Lelio Sozzini">Lelio Sozzini</a>'s commentary on John (1561)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> through to the increasing resistance to the miraculous among English Unitarians after 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>Affinities with the Christadelphian concept of the devil and/or demons are found in a range of early Jewish and later Christian sources such as: <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_ben_Uzziel" title="Jonathan ben Uzziel">Jonathan ben Uzziel</a> (100s AD);<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joshua_Ben_Karha" title="Joshua Ben Karha">Joshua Ben Karha</a> (135–160);<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levi_ben_Gershon" title="Levi ben Gershon">Levi ben Gershon</a> (d. 1344); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Kimchi" title="David Kimchi">David Kimchi</a> (1160); <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia ben Joseph</a> (892–942); <a href="/wiki/Shimon_ben_Lakish" title="Shimon ben Lakish">Shimon ben Lakish</a> (230–270),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Mede" title="Joseph Mede">Joseph Mede</a> (1640), <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Bauthumley" title="Jacob Bauthumley">Jacob Bauthumley</a> (1650), <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> (1651), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lodowick_Muggleton" title="Lodowick Muggleton">Lodowick Muggleton</a> (1669), Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthonie_van_Dale" title="Anthonie van Dale">Anthonie van Dale</a> (1685),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Balthasar_Bekker" title="Balthasar Bekker">Balthasar Bekker</a> (1695), <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_Thomasius" title="Christian Thomasius">Christian Thomasius</a> (1704), <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Ashley_Sykes" title="Arthur Ashley Sykes">Arthur Ashley Sykes</a> (1737), <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Lardner" title="Nathaniel Lardner">Nathaniel Lardner</a> (1742), Dr. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Mead" title="Richard Mead">Richard Mead</a> (1755), <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Farmer" title="Hugh Farmer">Hugh Farmer</a> (at least in the account of Christ's temptation; 1761), <a href="/wiki/William_Ashdowne" title="William Ashdowne">William Ashdowne</a> (1791),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Simpson_(Unitarian)" title="John Simpson (Unitarian)">John Simpson</a> (1804) and <a href="/wiki/John_Epps" title="John Epps">John Epps</a> (1842)</li>
</ul>
<p>Organised worship in England for those whose beliefs anticipated those of Christadelphians only truly became possible in 1779 when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Toleration_1689" title="Act of Toleration 1689">Act of Toleration 1689</a> was amended to permit denial of the Trinity, and only fully when property penalties were removed in the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Trinity_Act_1813" title="Doctrine of the Trinity Act 1813">Doctrine of the Trinity Act 1813</a>. This is only 35 years before John Thomas' 1849 lecture tour in Britain which attracted significant support from an existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Trinitarian" title="Non-Trinitarian">non-Trinitarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventist</a> base, particularly, initially, in Scotland where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arian" title="Arian">Arian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socinian" title="Socinian">Socinian</a>, and unitarian (with a small 'u' as distinct from the Unitarian Church of <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Lindsey" title="Theophilus Lindsey">Theophilus Lindsey</a>) views were prevalent.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices_and_worship">Practices and worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Practices and worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2142" data-file-width="2856" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG/220px-Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG/330px-Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG/440px-Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christadelphian_Chapel_Sign.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sign showing the service times of a Christadelphian ecclesia in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christadelphians are organised into local congregations, that commonly call themselves <i>ecclesias</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hyndman_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-hyndman-73">[73]</a></sup> which is taken from usage in the New Testament<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> and is Greek for <i>gathering of those summoned</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Congregational worship, which usually takes place on Sunday, centres on the remembrance of the death and celebration of the resurrection of Jesus Christ by the taking part in the "memorial service". Additional meetings are often organised for worship, prayer, preaching and Bible study.</p>
<p>Ecclesias are typically involved in preaching the gospel (<a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">evangelism</a>) in the form of public lectures on Bible teaching,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> college-style seminars on reading the Bible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> and Bible Reading Groups. Correspondence courses<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> are also used widely, particularly in areas where there is no established Christadelphian presence. Some ecclesias, organisations or individuals also preach through other media like video,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> podcasts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> and internet forums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> There are also a number of Bible Education/Learning Centres around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Only baptised (by complete immersion in water) believers are considered members of the ecclesia. Ordinarily, baptism follows someone making a "good confession" (cf. 1 Tim. 6:12) of their faith before two or three nominated elders of the ecclesia they are seeking to join. The good confession has to demonstrate a basic understanding of the main elements – "first principles" – of the faith of the community. The children of members are encouraged to attend Christadelphian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunday_Schools" title="Sunday Schools">Sunday Schools</a> and youth groups. Interaction between youth from different ecclesias is encouraged through regional and national youth gatherings. Many ecclesias organise holidays for young people, the most popular form in the UK being <a href="/wiki/Camping" title="Camping">camping</a> holidays and Youth Weekends such as Swanwick and others locally organised by different ecclesias.</p>
<p>Christadelphians understand the Bible to teach that male and female believers are equal in God's sight, and also that there is a distinction between the roles of male and female members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Women are typically not eligible to teach in formal gatherings of the ecclesia when male believers are present, are expected to cover their heads (using hat or scarf, etc.) during formal services, and do not sit on the main ecclesial arranging (organising) committees. They do, however: participate in other ecclesial and inter-ecclesial committees; participate in discussions; teach children in Sunday Schools as well as at home, teach other women and non-members; perform music; discuss and vote on business matters; and engage in the majority of other activities. Generally, at formal ecclesial and inter-ecclesial meetings the women wear head coverings when there are acts of worship and prayer.</p>
<p>There are ecclesially-accountable committees for co-ordinated <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">preaching</a>, youth and Sunday School work, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscientious_objection" title="Conscientious objection">conscientious objection</a> issues, care of the elderly, and humanitarian work. These do not have any legislative authority, and are wholly dependent upon ecclesial support. Ecclesias in an area may regularly hold joint activities combining youth groups, fellowship, preaching, and Bible study.</p>
<p>Christadelphians refuse to participate in any military (and police forces) because they are conscientious objectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gospelandstrife_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-gospelandstrife-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warandpolitics_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-warandpolitics-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Christadelphians do not vote in political elections, as they take direction from Romans 13:1–4, which they interpret as meaning that God puts into power those leaders He deems worthy. To vote for a candidate that does not win an election would be considered to vote against God's will. To avoid the risk of such conflict, Christadelphians abstain from voting.</p>
<p>There is a strong emphasis on personal Bible reading and study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> and many Christadelphians use the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Companion" title="Bible Companion">Bible Companion</a> to help them systematically read the Bible each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hymnody_and_music">Hymnody and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christadelphians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hymnody and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christadelphian_hymnals" title="Christadelphian hymnals">Christadelphian hymnals</a></div>
<p>Christadelphians are a <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">non-liturgical</a> denomination. Christadelphian ecclesias are autonomous and free to adopt whatever pattern of worship they choose. However, in the English-speaking world, there tends to be a great deal of uniformity in order of service and <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymnody</a>.</p>
<p>Christadelphian hymnody makes considerable use of the hymns of the Anglican and British Protestant traditions (even in US ecclesias the hymnody is typically more British than American).
